All SIFT information\xa0on this page is adapted from\xa0his materials with a\xa0[CC BY 4.0](https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/)\xa0license.\r\n\r\nDetermining if resources are credible is challenging. Use the\xa0SIFT method\xa0to help you analyze information, especially news or other online media.\r\n\r\n## S - Stop\r\n\r\n**Before you read or share an article or video, STOP\\!\u200b**\r\n\r\nBe aware of your emotional response to the headline or information in the article. Headlines are often meant to get clicks, and will do so by causing the reader to have a strong emotional response.\r\n\r\nBefore sharing, consider:\r\n\r\nWhat you already know about the topic. \u200b\r\n\r\nWhat you know about the source. Do you know it's reputation\\?\r\n\r\nBefore moving forward or sharing, use the other three moves: **I**nvestigate the Source, **F**ind Better Coverage, and **T**race Claims, Quotes, and Media back to the Original Context.\r\n\r\n## I - Investigate the Source\r\n\r\n**The next step before sharing is to Investigate the Source.**\r\n\r\nTake a moment to look up the author and source publishing the information.\r\n\r\nWhat can you find about the author/website creators\\? \u200b\r\n\r\nWhat is their mission\\? Do they have vested interests\\? \u200bWould their assessment be biased\\?\r\n\r\nDo they have authority in the area\\?\u200b\r\n\r\nUse [**lateral reading**](https://webliteracy.pressbooks.com/chapter/what-reading-laterally-means/). Go beyond the 'About Us' section on the organization's website and see what other, trusted sources say about the source.\u200b You can use Google or Wikipedia to investigate the source.\r\n\r\n**[Hovering](https://infodemic.blog/2020/02/16/lets-hover/)** is another technique to learn more about who is sharing information, especially on social media platforms such as Twitter.\r\n\r\n## F - Find Better Coverage\r\n\r\n**The next step is to Find Better Coverage or other sources that may or may not support the original claim.**\r\n\r\nAgain, use lateral reading to see if you can find other sources corroborating the same information or disputing it.\u200b\r\n\r\nWhat coverage is available on the topic\\?\xa0\r\n\r\nKeep track of trusted news sources.\r\n\r\nMany times, fact checkers have already looked into the claims. These fact-checkers are often nonpartisan, nonprofit websites that try to increase public knowledge and understanding by fact checking claims to see if they are based on fact or if they are biased/not supported by evidence.\r\n\r\n[FactCheck.org\u200b](https://www.factcheck.org/)\r\n\r\n[Snopes.com\u200b](https://www.snopes.com/)\r\n\r\n[Washington Post Fact Checker\u200b](https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/fact-checker/?utm_term=.cef7932feec8)\r\n\r\n[\xa0PolitiFact](https://www.politifact.com/)\r\n\r\n## T - Trace Claims, Quotes, and Media to their Original Context\r\n\r\n**The final step is to Trace Claims, Quotes, and Media to their Original Context.**\r\n\r\nWhen an article references a quote from an expert, or results of a research study, it is good practice to attempt to locate the original source of the information.\u200b Click through the links to follow the claims to the original source of information. Open up the original reporting sources listed in a bibliography if present\r\n\r\nWas the claim, quote, or media\xa0fairly represented\\?\r\n\r\nDoes the extracted information support the original claims in the research\\? \u200b\r\n\r\nIs information being cherry-picked to support an agenda or a bias\\?\u200b\r\n\r\nIs information being taken out of context\\?\u200b\r\n\r\nRemember, headlines, blog posts, or tweets may sensationalize facts to get more attention or clicks. \u200bRe-reporting may omit, misinterpret, or select certain facts to support biased claims. If the claim is taken from a source who took it from another source, important facts and contextual information can be left out. Make sure to read the claims in the original context in which they were presented.\r\n\r\nWhen in doubt, contact an expert – [like a librarian\\!\u200b](https://www.lib.uchicago.edu/research/help/ask-librarian/).", 'bodyText': "The SIFT Method\n\nThe SIFT method is an evaluation strategy developed by digital literacy expert, Mike Caulfield, to help determine whether online content can be trusted for credible or reliable sources of information. THE WORLD OF UNIVERSALS\r\n1. We shall therefore use the word 'universal' instead of the word 'idea', to describe what Plato meant. The essence of the sort of entity that Plato meant is that it is opposed to the particular things that are given in sensation. We speak of whatever is given in sensation, or is of the same nature as things given in sensation, as a particular; by opposition to this, a universal will be anything which may be shared by many particulars, and has those characteristics which, as we saw, distinguish justice and whiteness from just acts and white things.\r\n2. When we examine common words, we find that, broadly speaking, proper names stand for particulars, while other substantives, adjectives, prepositions, and verbs stand for universals. Pronouns stand for particulars, but are ambiguous: it is only by the context or the circumstances that we know what particulars they stand for. The word 'now' stands for a particular, namely the present moment; but like pronouns, it stands for an ambiguous particular, because the present is always changing.\r\n3. It will be seen that no sentence can be made up without at least one word which denotes a universal.Thus all truths involve universals, and all knowledge of truths involves acquaintance with universals.\r\n4. We shall find it convenient only to speak of things existing when they are in time, that is to say, when we can point to some time at which they exist (not excluding the possibility of their existing at all times). Thus thoughts and feelings, minds and physical objects exist. But universals do not exist in this sense; we shall say that they subsist or have being, where 'being' is opposed to 'existence' as being timeless. The world of universals, therefore, may also be described as the world of being. The world of being is unchangeable, rigid, exact, delightful to the mathematician, the logician, the builder of metaphysical systems, and all who love perfection more than life. The world of existence is fleeting, vague, without sharp boundaries, without any clear plan or arrangement, but it contains all thoughts and feelings, all the data of sense, and all physical objects, everything that can do either good or harm, everything that makes any difference to the value of life and the world. \r\n\r\n## CHAPTER X. ON OUR KNOWLEDGE OF UNIVERSALS\r\n1. In regard to one man's knowledge at a given time, universals, like particulars, may be divided into those known by acquaintance, those known only by description, and those not known either by acquaintance or by description.\r\n2. Universals of this sort may be called 'sensible qualities'. They can be apprehended with less effort of abstraction than any others, and they seem less removed from particulars than other universals are.\r\n3. I see successively a number of sense-data in which one part is to the left of another; I perceive, as in the case of different white patches, that all these sense-data have something in common, and by abstraction I find that what they have in common is a certain relation between their parts, namely the relation which I call 'being to the left of'. In this way I become acquainted with the universal relation.\r\n4. From either of these sources I can abstract the universal relation of before and after, just as I abstracted the universal relation 'being to the left of'. Thus time-relations, like space-relations, are among those with which we are acquainted.\r\n5. Another relation with which we become acquainted in much the same way is resemblance.\r\n6. Between universals, as between particulars, there are relations of which we may be immediately aware.Here we are dealing with a relation, namely 'greater than', between two relations. Our knowledge of such relations, though it requires more power of abstraction than is required for perceiving the qualities of sense-data, appears to be equally immediate, and (at least in some cases) equally indubitable. Thus there is immediate knowledge concerning universals as well as concerning sense-data.\r\n7. All a priori knowledge deals exclusively with the relations of universals. This proposition is of great importance, and goes a long way towards solving our previous difficulties concerning a priori knowledge.\r\n8. The only case in which it might seem, at first sight, as if our proposition were untrue, is the case in which an a priori proposition states that all of one class of particulars belong to some other class, or (what comes to the same thing) that all particulars having some one property also have some other. In this case it might seem as though we were dealing with the particulars that have the property rather than with the property.\r\n9. Thus although our general statement implies statements about particular couples, as soon as we know that there are such particular couples, yet it does not itself assert or imply that there are such particular couples, and thus fails to make any statement whatever about any actual particular couple. The statement made is about 'couple', the universal, and not about this or that couple.\r\n10. Hence, although our general proposition is a priori, all its applications to actual particulars involve experience and therefore contain an empirical element. In this way what seemed mysterious in our a priori knowledge is seen to have been based upon an error.\r\n11. Thus the difference between an a priori general proposition and an empirical generalization does not come in the meaning of the proposition; it comes in the nature of the evidence for it.the ultimate ground remains inductive, i.e. derived from instances, and not an a priori connexion of universals such as we have in logic and arithmetic.\r\n12. Two opposite points are to be observed concerning a priori general propositions. The first is that, if many particular instances are known, our general proposition may be arrived at in the first instance by induction, and the connexion of universals may be only subsequently perceived. \r\n13. The other point is more interesting, and of more philosophical importance. It is, that we may sometimes know a general proposition in cases where we do not know a single instance of it. \r\n14. This possibility, of knowledge of general propositions of which no instance can be given, is often denied, because it is not perceived that the knowledge of such propositions only requires a knowledge of the relations of universals, and does not require any knowledge of instances of the universals in question.\r\n15. We may now take a survey of **the sources of our knowledge**, as they have appeared in the course of our analysis. We have first to distinguish knowledge of things and knowledge of truths. In each there are two kinds, one immediate and one derivative. Our immediate knowledge of things, which we called acquaintance, consists of two sorts, according as the things known are particulars or universals. Among particulars, we have acquaintance with sense-data and (probably) with ourselves. Among universals, there seems to be no principle by which we can decide which can be known by acquaintance, but it is clear that among those that can be so known are sensible qualities, relations of space and time, similarity, and certain abstract logical universals. Our derivative knowledge of things, which we call knowledge by description, always involves both acquaintance with something and knowledge of truths. Our immediate knowledge of truths may be called intuitive knowledge, and the truths so known may be called self-evident truths. Among such truths are included those which merely state what is given in sense, and also certain abstract logical and arithmetical principles, and (though with less certainty) some ethical propositions. Our derivative knowledge of truths consists of everything that we can deduce from self-evident truths by the use of self-evident principles of deduction.\r\n16. If the above account is correct, all our knowledge of truths depends upon our intuitive knowledge. It therefore becomes important to consider the nature and scope of intuitive knowledge, in much the same way as, at an earlier stage, we considered the nature and scope of knowledge by acquaintance.\r\n\r\n## CHAPTER XI. ON INTUITIVE KNOWLEDGE\r\n1. There is a common impression that everything that we believe ought to be capable of proof, or at least of being shown to be highly probable. It is felt by many that a belief for which no reason can be given is an unreasonable belief. In the main, this view is just. \r\n2. But beyond that, there seems to be no further regress. The principle itself is constantly used in our reasoning, sometimes consciously, sometimes unconsciously; but there is no reasoning which, starting from some simpler self-evident principle, leads us to the principle of induction as its conclusion. And the same holds for other logical principles. Their truth is evident to us, and we employ them in constructing demonstrations; but they themselves, or at least some of them, are incapable of demonstration.\r\n3. It is usually through particular instances that we come to be able to see the general principle. Only those who are practised in dealing with abstractions can readily grasp a general principle without the help of instances.\r\n4. In addition to general principles, the other kind of self-evident truths are those immediately derived from sensation.Thus whatever self-evident truths may be obtained from our senses must be different from the sense-data from which they are obtained.\r\n5. It would seem that there are two kinds of self-evident truths of perception, though perhaps in the last analysis the two kinds may coalesce. First, there is the kind which simply asserts the existence of the sense-datum, without in any way analysing it.The other kind arises when the object of sense is complex, and we subject it to some degree of analysis.\r\n6. Another class of intuitive judgements, analogous to those of sense and yet quite distinct from them, are judgements of memory. There is some danger of confusion as to the nature of memory, owing to the fact that memory of an object is apt to be accompanied by an image of the object, and yet the image cannot be what constitutes memory.\r\n7. The case of memory, however, raises a difficulty, for it is notoriously fallacious, and thus throws doubt on the trustworthiness of intuitive judgements in general. This difficulty is no light one. But let us first narrow its scope as far as possible. Broadly speaking, memory is trustworthy in proportion to the vividness of the experience and to its nearness in time. \r\n8. Thus the first answer to the difficulty of fallacious memory is to say that memory has degrees of self-evidence, and that these correspond to the degrees of its trustworthiness, reaching a limit of perfect self-evidence and perfect trustworthiness in our memory of events which are recent and vivid.\r\n9. It would seem, however, that there are cases of very firm belief in a memory which is wholly false. It is probable that, in these cases, what is really remembered, in the sense of being immediately before the mind, is something other than what is falsely believed in, though something generally associated with it.\r\n10. One important point about self-evidence is made clear by the case of memory, and that is, that self-evidence has degrees: it is not a quality which is simply present or absent, but a quality which may be more or less present, in gradations ranging from absolute certainty down to an almost imperceptible faintness. Truths of perception and some of the principles of logic have the very highest degree of self-evidence; truths of immediate memory have an almost equally high degree. The inductive principle has less self-evidence than some of the other principles of logic, such as 'what follows from a true premiss must be true'. Memories have a diminishing self-evidence as they become remoter and fainter; the truths of logic and mathematics have (broadly speaking) less self-evidence as they become more complicated. Judgements of intrinsic ethical or aesthetic value are apt to have some self-evidence, but not much.\r\n11. Degrees of self-evidence are important in the theory of knowledge, since, if propositions may (as seems likely) have some degree of self-evidence without being true, it will not be necessary to abandon all connexion between self-evidence and truth, but merely to say that, where there is a conflict, the more self-evident proposition is to be retained and the less self-evident rejected.\r\n12. It seems, however, highly probable that two different notions are combined in 'self-evidence' as above explained; that one of them, which corresponds to the highest degree of self-evidence, is really an infallible guarantee of truth, while the other, which corresponds to all the other degrees, does not give an infallible guarantee, but only a greater or less presumption. \r\n\r\n## CHAPTER XII. TRUTH AND FALSEHOOD\r\n1. Our knowledge of truths, unlike our knowledge of things, has an opposite, namely error. So far as things are concerned, we may know them or not know them, but there is no positive state of mind which can be described as erroneous knowledge of things, so long, at any rate, as we confine ourselves to knowledge by acquaintance.we are asking what is meant by the question whether a belief is true or false. It is to be hoped that a clear answer to this question may help us to obtain an answer to the question what beliefs are true, but for the present we ask only 'What is truth?' and 'What is falsehood?' not 'What beliefs are true?' and 'What beliefs are false?'\r\n2. There are three points to observe in the attempt to discover the nature of truth, three requisites which any theory must fulfil.\r\n3. Our theory of truth must be such as to admit of its opposite, falsehood. \r\n4. It seems fairly evident that if there were no beliefs there could be no falsehood, and no truth either, in the sense in which truth is correlative to falsehood.In fact, truth and falsehood are properties of beliefs and statements: hence a world of mere matter, since it would contain no beliefs or statements, would also contain no truth or falsehood.\r\n5. But, as against what we have just said, it is to be observed that the truth or falsehood of a belief always depends upon something which lies outside the belief itself. Hence, although truth and falsehood are properties of beliefs, they are properties dependent upon the relations of the beliefs to other things, not upon any internal quality of the beliefs.\r\n6. ", 'bodyText': "CHAPTER IX. THE WORLD OF UNIVERSALS\n\nWe shall therefore use the word 'universal' instead of the word 'idea', to describe what Plato meant. The world of being is unchangeable, rigid, exact, delightful to the mathematician, the logician, the builder of metaphysical systems, and all who love perfection more than life. The world of existence is fleeting, vague, without sharp boundaries, without any clear plan or arrangement, but it contains all thoughts and feelings, all the data of sense, and all physical objects, everything that can do either good or harm, everything that makes any difference to the value of life and the world.\n\nCHAPTER X. ON OUR KNOWLEDGE OF UNIVERSALS\n\nIn regard to one man's knowledge at a given time, universals, like particulars, may be divided into those known by acquaintance, those known only by description, and those not known either by acquaintance or by description.\nUniversals of this sort may be called 'sensible qualities'. They can be apprehended with less effort of abstraction than any others, and they seem less removed from particulars than other universals are.\nI see successively a number of sense-data in which one part is to the left of another; I perceive, as in the case of different white patches, that all these sense-data have something in common, and by abstraction I find that what they have in common is a certain relation between their parts, namely the relation which I call 'being to the left of'. In this way I become acquainted with the universal relation.\nFrom either of these sources I can abstract the universal relation of before and after, just as I abstracted the universal relation 'being to the left of'. Thus time-relations, like space-relations, are among those with which we are acquainted.\nAnother relation with which we become acquainted in much the same way is resemblance.\nBetween universals, as between particulars, there are relations of which we may be immediately aware.Here we are dealing with a relation, namely 'greater than', between two relations. Our knowledge of such relations, though it requires more power of abstraction than is required for perceiving the qualities of sense-data, appears to be equally immediate, and (at least in some cases) equally indubitable. Thus there is immediate knowledge concerning universals as well as concerning sense-data.\nAll a priori knowledge deals exclusively with the relations of universals. This proposition is of great importance, and goes a long way towards solving our previous difficulties concerning a priori knowledge.\nThe only case in which it might seem, at first sight, as if our proposition were untrue, is the case in which an a priori proposition states that all of one class of particulars belong to some other class, or (what comes to the same thing) that all particulars having some one property also have some other. In this case it might seem as though we were dealing with the particulars that have the property rather than with the property.\nThus although our general statement implies statements about particular couples, as soon as we know that there are such particular couples, yet it does not itself assert or imply that there are such particular couples, and thus fails to make any statement whatever about any actual particular couple. The statement made is about 'couple', the universal, and not about this or that couple.\nHence, although our general proposition is a priori, all its applications to actual particulars involve experience and therefore contain an empirical element. In this way what seemed mysterious in our a priori knowledge is seen to have been based upon an error.\nThus the difference between an a priori general proposition and an empirical generalization does not come in the meaning of the proposition; it comes in the nature of the evidence for it.the ultimate ground remains inductive, i.e. derived from instances, and not an a priori connexion of universals such as we have in logic and arithmetic.\nTwo opposite points are to be observed concerning a priori general propositions. The first is that, if many particular instances are known, our general proposition may be arrived at in the first instance by induction, and the connexion of universals may be only subsequently perceived.\nThe other point is more interesting, and of more philosophical importance. It is, that we may sometimes know a general proposition in cases where we do not know a single instance of it.\nThis possibility, of knowledge of general propositions of which no instance can be given, is often denied, because it is not perceived that the knowledge of such propositions only requires a knowledge of the relations of universals, and does not require any knowledge of instances of the universals in question.\nWe may now take a survey of the sources of our knowledge, as they have appeared in the course of our analysis. We have first to distinguish knowledge of things and knowledge of truths. In each there are two kinds, one immediate and one derivative. Our immediate knowledge of things, which we called acquaintance, consists of two sorts, according as the things known are particulars or universals. Among particulars, we have acquaintance with sense-data and (probably) with ourselves. Among universals, there seems to be no principle by which we can decide which can be known by acquaintance, but it is clear that among those that can be so known are sensible qualities, relations of space and time, similarity, and certain abstract logical universals. Our derivative knowledge of things, which we call knowledge by description, always involves both acquaintance with something and knowledge of truths. Our immediate knowledge of truths may be called intuitive knowledge, and the truths so known may be called self-evident truths. Among such truths are included those which merely state what is given in sense, and also certain abstract logical and arithmetical principles, and (though with less certainty) some ethical propositions. Our derivative knowledge of truths consists of everything that we can deduce from self-evident truths by the use of self-evident principles of deduction.\nIf the above account is correct, all our knowledge of truths depends upon our intuitive knowledge. It therefore becomes important to consider the nature and scope of intuitive knowledge, in much the same way as, at an earlier stage, we considered the nature and scope of knowledge by acquaintance.\n\nCHAPTER XI. ON INTUITIVE KNOWLEDGE\n\nThere is a common impression that everything that we believe ought to be capable of proof, or at least of being shown to be highly probable. It is felt by many that a belief for which no reason can be given is an unreasonable belief. In the main, this view is just.\nBut beyond that, there seems to be no further regress. The principle itself is constantly used in our reasoning, sometimes consciously, sometimes unconsciously; but there is no reasoning which, starting from some simpler self-evident principle, leads us to the principle of induction as its conclusion. And the same holds for other logical principles. Their truth is evident to us, and we employ them in constructing demonstrations; but they themselves, or at least some of them, are incapable of demonstration.\nIt is usually through particular instances that we come to be able to see the general principle. Only those who are practised in dealing with abstractions can readily grasp a general principle without the help of instances.\nIn addition to general principles, the other kind of self-evident truths are those immediately derived from sensation.Thus whatever self-evident truths may be obtained from our senses must be different from the sense-data from which they are obtained.\nIt would seem that there are two kinds of self-evident truths of perception, though perhaps in the last analysis the two kinds may coalesce. First, there is the kind which simply asserts the existence of the sense-datum, without in any way analysing it.The other kind arises when the object of sense is complex, and we subject it to some degree of analysis.\nAnother class of intuitive judgements, analogous to those of sense and yet quite distinct from them, are judgements of memory. There is some danger of confusion as to the nature of memory, owing to the fact that memory of an object is apt to be accompanied by an image of the object, and yet the image cannot be what constitutes memory.\nThe case of memory, however, raises a difficulty, for it is notoriously fallacious, and thus throws doubt on the trustworthiness of intuitive judgements in general. This difficulty is no light one. But let us first narrow its scope as far as possible. Broadly speaking, memory is trustworthy in proportion to the vividness of the experience and to its nearness in time.\nThus the first answer to the difficulty of fallacious memory is to say that memory has degrees of self-evidence, and that these correspond to the degrees of its trustworthiness, reaching a limit of perfect self-evidence and perfect trustworthiness in our memory of events which are recent and vivid.\nIt would seem, however, that there are cases of very firm belief in a memory which is wholly false. It is probable that, in these cases, what is really remembered, in the sense of being immediately before the mind, is something other than what is falsely believed in, though something generally associated with it.\nOne important point about self-evidence is made clear by the case of memory, and that is, that self-evidence has degrees: it is not a quality which is simply present or absent, but a quality which may be more or less present, in gradations ranging from absolute certainty down to an almost imperceptible faintness. Truths of perception and some of the principles of logic have the very highest degree of self-evidence; truths of immediate memory have an almost equally high degree. The inductive principle has less self-evidence than some of the other principles of logic, such as 'what follows from a true premiss must be true'. Memories have a diminishing self-evidence as they become remoter and fainter; the truths of logic and mathematics have (broadly speaking) less self-evidence as they become more complicated. Judgements of intrinsic ethical or aesthetic value are apt to have some self-evidence, but not much.\nDegrees of self-evidence are important in the theory of knowledge, since, if propositions may (as seems likely) have some degree of self-evidence without being true, it will not be necessary to abandon all connexion between self-evidence and truth, but merely to say that, where there is a conflict, the more self-evident proposition is to be retained and the less self-evident rejected.\nIt seems, however, highly probable that two different notions are combined in 'self-evidence' as above explained; that one of them, which corresponds to the highest degree of self-evidence, is really an infallible guarantee of truth, while the other, which corresponds to all the other degrees, does not give an infallible guarantee, but only a greater or less presumption.\n\nCHAPTER XII. TRUTH AND FALSEHOOD\n\nOur knowledge of truths, unlike our knowledge of things, has an opposite, namely error. So far as things are concerned, we may know them or not know them, but there is no positive state of mind which can be described as erroneous knowledge of things, so long, at any rate, as we confine ourselves to knowledge by acquaintance.we are asking what is meant by the question whether a belief is true or false. It is to be hoped that a clear answer to this question may help us to obtain an answer to the question what beliefs are true, but for the present we ask only 'What is truth?' and 'What is falsehood?' not 'What beliefs are true?' and 'What beliefs are false?'\nThere are three points to observe in the attempt to discover the nature of truth, three requisites which any theory must fulfil.\nOur theory of truth must be such as to admit of its opposite, falsehood.\nIt seems fairly evident that if there were no beliefs there could be no falsehood, and no truth either, in the sense in which truth is correlative to falsehood.In fact, truth and falsehood are properties of beliefs and statements: hence a world of mere matter, since it would contain no beliefs or statements, would also contain no truth or falsehood.\nBut, as against what we have just said, it is to be observed that the truth or falsehood of a belief always depends upon something which lies outside the belief itself. Hence, although truth and falsehood are properties of beliefs, they are properties dependent upon the relations of the beliefs to other things, not upon any internal quality of the beliefs.", 'bodyHTML': '<h2 dir="auto">CHAPTER IX. THE WORLD OF UNIVERSALS</h2>\n<ol dir="auto">\n<li>We shall therefore use the word \'universal\' instead of the word \'idea\', to describe what Plato meant. The essence of the sort of entity that Plato meant is that it is opposed to the particular things that are given in sensation. We speak of whatever is given in sensation, or is of the same nature as things given in sensation, as a particular; by opposition to this, a universal will be anything which may be shared by many particulars, and has those characteristics which, as we saw, distinguish justice and whiteness from just acts and white things.</li>\n<li>When we examine common words, we find that, broadly speaking, proper names stand for particulars, while other substantives, adjectives, prepositions, and verbs stand for universals. Pronouns stand for particulars, but are ambiguous: it is only by the context or the circumstances that we know what particulars they stand for. The word \'now\' stands for a particular, namely the present moment; but like pronouns, it stands for an ambiguous particular, because the present is always changing.</li>\n<li>It will be seen that no sentence can be made up without at least one word which denotes a universal.Thus all truths involve universals, and all knowledge of truths involves acquaintance with universals.</li>\n<li>We shall find it convenient only to speak of things existing when they are in time, that is to say, when we can point to some time at which they exist (not excluding the possibility of their existing at all times). Thus thoughts and feelings, minds and physical objects exist. But universals do not exist in this sense; we shall say that they subsist or have being, where \'being\' is opposed to \'existence\' as being timeless. The world of universals, therefore, may also be described as the world of being. The world of being is unchangeable, rigid, exact, delightful to the mathematician, the logician, the builder of metaphysical systems, and all who love perfection more than life. The world of existence is fleeting, vague, without sharp boundaries, without any clear plan or arrangement, but it contains all thoughts and feelings, all the data of sense, and all physical objects, everything that can do either good or harm, everything that makes any difference to the value of life and the world.</li>\n</ol>\n<h2 dir="auto">CHAPTER X. We are afraid of rethinking answers and the idea of rethinking.</p>\n<h2 dir="auto">INDIVIDUAL RETHINKING:HOW TO OPEN OUR MIND TO RETHINK</h2>\n<h3 dir="auto">THINK LIKE A SCIENTIST</h3>\n<p dir="auto">Run experiments to test hypotheses and find knowledge</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">AVOID THINKING LIKE</h3>\n<ul dir="auto">\n<li>PREACHER We deliver sermons to protect and premote our ideals.</li>\n<li>PROSECUTOR We recognize flaws in other peoples reasoning to prove them wrong and win our case.</li>\n<li>POLITICIAN We campaign and lobby for the approval of our comstituents.</li>\n</ul>\n<h3 dir="auto">BEWARE OF THE MOUNT STUPID</h3>\n<p dir="auto">The danger lies not only in a lack of competence, but also in an overestimation of competence.</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">GET INTO TASK CONFLICTS</h3>\n<p dir="auto">Conflicts of ideas and opinions. Prevent it from turning into an emotional conflict. Well-performing groups do not hesitate to present opposing perspectives.</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">ATTAIN A CONFIDENT HUMILITY</h3>\n<h4 dir="auto">CONFIDENCE</h4>\n<p dir="auto">Armchair<br>\nquarterback<br>\nsyndrome</p>\n<h4 dir="auto">COMPETENCE</h4>\n<p dir="auto">Impostor<br>\nsyndrome</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">HAVE A CHALLENGING NETWORK</h3>\n<p dir="auto">A group of people we trust to point out our blind spots and help us overcome our weaknesses.</p>\n<h2 dir="auto">INTERPERSONAL RETHINKING</h2>\n<p dir="auto">HOW TO ENCOURAGE OTHERS TO RETHINK THEIR CONCEPTS</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">DEBATE LIKE A DANCE</h3>\n<p dir="auto">A good debate is not a<br>\nbattle, it\'s more like a dance<br>\nwithout choreography.</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">LEARN TO DEBATE</h3>\n<p dir="auto">Find a common denominator, focus on few arguments, avoid getting into an attack-defense spiral, ask lots of questions.</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">EXERCISE COUNTERFACTUAL THINKING</h3>\n<p dir="auto">Imagine how the circumstances of our lives could have unfolded differently.</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">PRACTICE MOTIVATIONAL INTERVIEWS</h3>\n<p dir="auto">Ask open-ended questions, listen reflectively, affirm the other person\'s desire and ability to change.</p>\n<h2 dir="auto">COLLECTIVE RETHINKING</h2>\n<p dir="auto">HOW TO CREATE COMMUNITIES OF LIFELONG LEARNERS</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">AVOID BINARY BIAS</h3>\n<p dir="auto">When it comes to complex issues, knowing the other side\'s opinions isn\'t enough. The opposing arguments do not change our view.<br>\nGenerates polarization.</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">TEACH HOW TO CHECK THE FACTS</h3>\n<p dir="auto">Teach children to question information instead of simply consuming it. Reject rank as a proxy for reliability</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">TEACH HOW TO THINK LIKE A SCIENTIST</h3>\n<p dir="auto">Identifying problems, developing hypotheses and devising their own experiments to test them.</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">BUILT A CULTURE OF LEARNING</h3>\n<p dir="auto">Learning culture, organizations innovate more and make fewer mistakes. "3.信息系统的考查(10分)"\n必修课程的模块2: 信息系统与社会\n\n命题思路: 信息系统内容较多，重点考查信息系统的搭建和应用。为降低难度，采取修补的思路，采用拖放技术，在读懂题目的基础上，补全给定的信息系统结构图。\n\n题目1\n中学生100米跑步可以提高身体协调性，加强肌肉力量。现在请你设计一个算法，以男生为例将达标成绩保存到变量b中，输入100米跑步成绩并保存到变量f中，如达标输出“恭喜达标”，否则输出“未达标，继续努力”。（参照标准：男生100米达标成绩14.9秒。）\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案\n\n\n题目2\n为了更好的服务居民，某小区超市开展了送货上门活动，单次购买物资金额（用变量a表示）不超过50元时，收取送货费3元；单次购买物资金额超过50元时，收取送货费1元，请你设计并完善“计算订单总费用（用变量b表示）”算法的流程图。\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案\n\n\n题目3\n试题描述：夏天使用空调，设定温度后，当室内温度高于设定温度时，空调开始制冷；而当室内温度降至设定温度时，则转为通风状态。设定温度为t1，检测室内温度为t2，模式开关为m。根据室内温度模拟空调控制自动送风模式，现请你设计该问题的算法流程图。\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案\n\n\n题目4\n自动售货机的投币系统功能为：输入商品的价格p、用户的投币金额m，如果投币金额大于等于商品价格，则计算并输出找零金额yu；否则输出“零钱不足”。现给出解决该问题的部分算法流程图，请你进行补充完善。\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案\n\n\n题目5\n太原市公交车票刷卡为每次0.5元。刷卡时，若余额不足0.5元，提示""请投币""；若余额0.5元以上(含0.5元)，直接扣除本次乘车费用；扣除后余额不足5元提示""余额即将不足""，若余额5元以上(含5元)，提示""欢迎乘车""。请描述该算法。\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案\n\n\n题目6\n为了提倡居民节约用电，电力公司制订了如下的阶梯电价收费办法：每月用电量在170度及以下时，电价为0.477元/度；每月用电量在170度以上时，电价为0.527元/度。请你设计完善“计算电费”的算法流程图，其中变量n为正数：表示月用电量(度)， money：表示电费(元).\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案\n\n\n题目7\n某电商平台搞活动，同一件商品购买不超过5件时按原价出售，超过5件时打7折出售，需要通过输入商品单价p和数量n计算并输出总价s。现给出解决该问题的部分算法流程图，请你进行补充完善。\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案\n\n\n题目8\n征兵时，男性身高要求在160cm以上，现有一台身高测量仪，输入标准身高，即可判断受测人身高是否合格。当测得身高大于160cm时，机器语音播报“身高合格，请测量体重”，否则播报“很遗憾，身高不合格""。现设身高变量为h，请完善该任务的算法流程图。\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案\n\n\n题目9\n为唤起公众的节水意识，加强水资源保护，其市通过价格调控手段来达到节水的目的，其生活用水收费标准为∶每户每月用水量未超过12立方米时，每立方米收费2.8元；超过12立方米的部分，每立方米收费4.2元。请你完善该问题的算法流程图。\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案\n\n\n题目10\n对任意输入的两个整数x和y，找出并输出其中的较大值。x和y分别表示两个任意整数， big表示输出的较大值、现清你设计该问题的算法流程图。\n【操作要求】\n\n（1）请使用系统提供的图形，将流程图补充完整。\n（2）拖动符号区的图形，到中央区的流程图中。\n（3）输入相应内容，使之正确表达算法。\n\n\n中央流程区\n\n\n答案', 'bodyHTML': '<blockquote>\n<p 题目要求\r\n编程实现人机互动猜1到100之间整数游戏。\r\n计算机随机生成一个数，游戏参与人有5次猜数机会，每次在程序界面输入所猜的数，游戏程序会给予相应提示“猜大了！”或“猜小了！”\r\n猜对游戏直接结束，如5次尝试都不对游戏结束。请你阅读下列程序段，根据题意，实现游戏功能。\r\n【操作要求】\r\n1. 补充完善下划线处的内容使程序语句完整。(下划线须删去)\r\n2. 找出程序中的两处错误并改正。\r\n3. 运行程序，输入你猜的数字，验证结果是否正确。\r\n### 代码调试区 [在线调试](https://pythontutor.com/visualize.html#code=import%20random%0Anum%20%3D%20random.randint%281,%20100%29%0Aprint%28%22**********%E6%B8%B8%E6%88%8F%E5%BC%80%E5%A7%8B**********%22%29%0Afor%20i%20in%20range%281,%20___%29%3A%0A%20%20%20%20n%20%3D%20___%28input%28%22%E8%AF%B7%E8%BE%93%E5%85%A5%E4%BD%A0%E7%8C%9C%E7%9A%84%E6%95%B0%EF%BC%9A%22%29%29%0A%20%20%20%20if%20n%20%3D%3D%20num%3A%0A%20%20%20%20%20%20%20%20print%28%22%E6%81%AD%E5%96%9C%E4%BD%A0%EF%BC%8C%E7%8C%9C%E5%AF%B9%E4%BA%86%EF%BC%81%22%29%0A%20%20%20%20%20%20%20%20break%0A%20%20%20%20elif%20n%20%3C%20num%3A%0A%20%20%20%20%20%20%20%20print%28%22%E7%8C%9C%E5%B0%8F%E4%BA%86%EF%BC%81%22%29%0A%20%20%20%20elif%3A%0A%20%20%20%20print%28%22%E7%8C%9C%E5%A4%A7%E4%BA%86%EF%BC%81%22%29%0Aprint%28%22******%E6%B8%B8%E6%88%8F%E7%BB%93%E6%9D%9F******%22%29&cumulative=false&heapPrimitives=nevernest&mode=edit&origin=opt-frontend.js&py=3&rawInputLstJSON=%5B%5D&textReferences=false)\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\nimport random\r\nnum = random.randint(1, 100)\r\nprint("**********游戏开始**********")\r\nfor i in range(1, ___):\r\n    n = ___(input("请输入你猜的数："))\r\n    if n == num:\r\n        print("恭喜你，猜对了！")\r\n        break\r\n    elif n < num:\r\n        print("猜小了！")\r\n    elif:\r\n    print("猜大了！")\r\nprint("******游戏结束******")\r\n```\r\n### 提示\r\n1. 请注意，代码中有一个错误：在elif语句之后应该有一个条件，但是在elif后面没有条件。正确的应该是else，而不是elif。此外，for循环中的____应该被替换为用户希望的尝试次数。例如，如果想让用户有10次机会猜测，就应该写成range(1, 11)\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\nimport random   # 导入random模块以使用其函数\r\n# 使用random模块的randint函数生成一个1到100之间的随机数，并将其赋值给变量num\r\nnum = random.randint(1, 100)\r\nprint("**********游戏开始**********")   # 打印游戏开始的信息\r\nfor i in range(1, ___):   # 使用for循环，让用户有多次机会猜测数字，____处应填写尝试的次数\r\n    n = ___(input("请输入你猜的数："))   # 获取用户输入的数字，并转换为整数类型\r\n    if n == num:   # 如果用户猜的数字与程序生成的随机数相同\r\n        print("恭喜你，猜对了！")   # 打印恭喜信息\r\n        break   # 退出循环\r\n    elif n < num:   # 如果用户猜的数字小于程序生成的随机数\r\n        print("猜小了！")   # 提示用户猜小了\r\n    elif:   # 如果用户猜的数字大于程序生成的随机数\r\n    print("猜大了！")   # 提示用户猜大了\r\nprint("******游戏结束******")   # 当循环结束时，打印游戏结束的信息\r\n```\r\n### 答案\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\nimport random   # 导入random模块以使用其函数\r\n# 使用random模块的randint函数生成一个1到100之间的随机数，并将其赋值给变量num\r\nnum = random.randint(1, 100)\r\nprint("**********游戏开始**********")   # 打印游戏开始的信息\r\nfor i in range(1, 6):   # 使用for循环，让用户有多次机会猜测数字，____处应填写尝试的次数         #填空1\r\n    n = int(input("请输入你猜的数："))   # 获取用户输入的数字，并转换为整数类型                 #填空2\r\n    if n == num:   # 如果用户猜的数字与程序生成的随机数相同\r\n        print("恭喜你，猜对了！")   # 打印恭喜信息\r\n        break   # 退出循环\r\n    elif n < num:   # 如果用户猜的数字小于程序生成的随机数\r\n        print("猜小了！")   # 提示用户猜小了\r\n    else:   # 如果用户猜的数字大于程序生成的随机数                                             #错误1\r\n        print("猜大了！")   # 提示用户猜大了                                                  #错误2\r\nprint("******游戏结束******")   # 当循环结束时，打印游戏结束的信息\r\n```\r\n\r\n## 题目2\r\n### 题目要求\r\n为切实增强学生身体素质，学校每学期进行体质健康测试。其中一个项目为“一分种垫排球”，考核规则为: 每人三次机会，\r\n需有两次连续垫球次数满35次(包含35)方能通过考核。请你完善程序，输入学生每次的垫球次数，并判断是否通过考核。\r\n【操作要示】\r\n(1)补充完善下划线处的内容使程序语句完整\r\n(2)找出程序中的两处错误并改正\r\n(3)运行程序，当依次输入每次的垫球次数为36、32、38时，检查输出结果是否为"恭喜你，已通过考核!”。\r\n### 代码调试区 [在线调试](https://pythontutor.com/visualize.html#code=count%20%3D%200%0Afor%20time%20in%20range%281,%203,%201%29%3A%0A%20%20%20%20dig%20%3D%20int%28input%28%22%E8%AF%B7%E8%BE%93%E5%85%A5%E4%BD%A0%E7%9A%84%E5%9E%AB%E7%90%83%E6%AC%A1%E6%95%B0%22%29%29%0A%20%20%20%20if________%3A%0A%20%20%20%20count%20%3D%20________%0Aif%20count%20%3E%3D%202%3A%0A%20%20%20%20print%28%22%E6%81%AD%E5%96%9C%E4%BD%A0%EF%BC%8C%E9%80%9A%E8%BF%87%E8%80%83%E6%A0%B8%22%29%0Aelse%0A%20%20%20%20print%28%22%E6%8A%B1%E6%AD%89%EF%BC%8C%E6%9C%AA%E9%80%9A%E8%BF%87%E8%80%83%E6%A0%B8%22%29&cumulative=false&heapPrimitives=nevernest&mode=edit&origin=opt-frontend.js&py=3&rawInputLstJSON=%5B%5D&textReferences=false)\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\ncount = 0\r\nfor time in range(1, 3, 1):\r\n    dig = int(input("请输入你的垫球次数"))\r\n    if________:\r\n    count = ________\r\nif count >= 2:\r\n    print("恭喜你，通过考核")\r\nelse\r\n    print("抱歉，未通过考核")\r\n```\r\n### 提示\r\n2. 请注意，if语句后面的条件和count变量的更新需要根据实际情况来填写。在这个例子中，我假设如果用户输入的垫球次数大于或等于35次，就认为是一次成功的垫球，并更新计数器。如果用户在两次机会中都达到了这个标准，程序就会恭喜他们通过考核。如果没有达到，就会告诉他们未通过考核。这个标准（5次）可以根据实际需要进行调整。\r\n\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\ncount = 0   # 初始化计数器为0\r\nfor time in range(1, 3, 1):   # 使用for循环，范围从1到2，每次增加1\r\n    dig = int(input("请输入你的垫球次数"))   # 通过input函数获取用户输入的垫球次数，并将其转换为整数类型\r\n    if________:   # 这里应该有一个条件判断语句，例如：如果输入的次数大于等于35，则计数器加1\r\n    count = ________   # 如果满足条件，计数器加1\r\nif count >= 2:   # 如果计数器的值大于或等于2\r\n    print("恭喜你，通过考核")  # 则打印“恭喜你，通过考核”\r\nelse   # 否则\r\n    print("抱歉，未通过考核")  # 打印“抱歉，未通过考核”\r\n```\r\n### 答案\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\ncount = 0   # 初始化计数器为0\r\nfor time in range(1, 3, 1):   # 使用for循环，范围从1到2，每次增加1\r\n    dig = int(input("请输入你的垫球次数"))   # 通过input函数获取用户输入的垫球次数，并将其转换为整数类型\r\n    if dig>=35:   # 这里应该有一个条件判断语句，例如：如果输入的次数大于等于35，则计数器加1                 #填空1\r\n        count = count+1   # 如果满足条件，计数器加1                                                     #填空2  #错误1\r\nif count >= 2:   # 如果计数器的值大于或等于2\r\n    print("恭喜你，通过考核")  # 则打印“恭喜你，通过考核”\r\nelse:   # 否则                                                                                         #错误2\r\n    print("抱歉，未通过考核")  # 打印“抱歉，未通过考核”\r\n```\r\n\r\n## 题目3\r\n### 题目要求\r\n某地发生了一起肇事车辆逃追事故，据调查发现如下线索:①车牌号为晋W 652?6，\r\n其中车牌号后五位全部由数字组成，且倒数第二位数字模糊不清;②这个五位数是53的倍数。\r\n请你阅读下列程序段，根据上述线索，找出符合条件的车牌号。\r\n【操作要求】 print(”该事故可能涉及到的车辆车牌共有:"count,"个") \r\n(1)补充完善下划线处的内容使程序语句完整。\r\n(2)找出程序中的两处惜误并改正。\r\n(3)运行程序，直至输出正确的结果,以协助交警队事故科快速处理该事故。\r\n### 代码调试区 [在线调试](https://pythontutor.com/visualize.html#code=______%0Afor%20i%20in%20range%280,___,%201%29%3A%0A%20%20%20%20t%3D65206%2Bi*100%20%20%20%20%0A%20%20%20%20if%20t%2553%3D0%3A%20%20%20%20%20%20%20%20%0A%20%20%20%20%20%20%20count%2B%3D1%20%20%20%20%20%20%20%23%E7%94%A8count%E8%AE%A1%E6%95%B0%20%0A%20%20%20%20%20%20%20print%28%22%E8%AF%A5%E4%BA%8B%E6%95%85%E5%8F%AF%E8%83%BD%E6%B6%89%E5%8F%8A%E5%88%B0%E7%9A%84%E8%BD%A6%E8%BE%86%E8%BD%A6%E7%89%8C%E5%8F%B7%E7%A0%81%E4%B8%BA%3A%E6%99%8BW%22,t%29%0Aprint%28%22%E8%AF%A5%E4%BA%8B%E6%95%85%E5%8F%AF%E8%83%BD%E6%B6%89%E5%8F%8A%E5%88%B0%E7%9A%84%E8%BD%A6%E8%BE%86%E8%BD%A6%E7%89%8C%E5%85%B1%E6%9C%89%22,count,%22%E4%B8%AA%22%29&cumulative=false&heapPrimitives=nevernest&mode=edit&origin=opt-frontend.js&py=3&rawInputLstJSON=%5B%5D&textReferences=false)\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\n______\r\nfor i in range(0,___, 1):\r\n    t=65206+i*100    \r\n    if t%53=0:        \r\n       count+=1       #用count计数 \r\n       print("该事故可能涉及到的车辆车牌号码为:晋W",t)\r\nprint("该事故可能涉及到的车辆车牌共有",count,"个")\r\n\r\n```\r\n### 提示\r\n3. 请注意，代码中的if t%53=0:应该是if t%53 == 0:，因为在Python中，==用于比较操作符，而=用于赋值操作。此外，for循环的范围___需要根据实际情况来确定，例如，如果要检查从65206到65306之间的车牌号码，可以将___替换为1。如果要检查更大范围内的车牌号码，可以相应地增加这个数字。\r\n\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\n______   # 初始化计数器为0\r\nfor i in range(0, ___, 1):   # 使用for循环遍历一个范围内的数字，这里的范围需要根据实际情况填写\r\n    t = 65206+i*100       # 计算可能涉及事故的车牌号码\r\n    if t % 53 = 0:           # 如果车牌号码除以53的余数为0，则认为该车牌号码满足条件\r\n       count += 1  # 用count计数    # 满足条件的车牌号码计数加1\r\n       print("该事故可能涉及到的车辆车牌号码为:晋W", t)   # 打印出满足条件的车牌号码\r\nprint("该事故可能涉及到的车辆车牌共有", count, "个")   # 最后打印出满足条件的车牌号码总数\r\n```\r\n### 答案\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\ncount=0   # 初始化计数器为0                                                                         #填空1\r\nfor i in range(0, 10, 1):   # 使用for循环遍历一个范围内的数字，这里的范围需要根据实际情况填写           #填空2\r\n    t = 65206+i*10       # 计算可能涉及事故的车牌号码                                                #错误1\r\n    if t % 53 == 0:           # 如果车牌号码除以53的余数为0，则认为该车牌号码满足条件                  #错误2\r\n       count += 1  # 用count计数    # 满足条件的车牌号码计数加1\r\n       print("该事故可能涉及到的车辆车牌号码为:晋W", t)   # 打印出满足条件的车牌号码\r\nprint("该事故可能涉及到的车辆车牌共有", count, "个")   # 最后打印出满足条件的车牌号码总数\r\n```\r\n## 题目4\r\n### 题目要求\r\n身体质量指数(BMI)是衡量人体肥胖程度的重要标准，其计算方法为身体质量指数等于体重(单位:kg)除以身高(单位:m)的平方。\r\n下表是高二男生BMI数值对应的等级表。请你根据提供的程序段输入身高和体重，计算BMI的值并判断BMI的等级。\r\nBMI数值范围\tBMI数值范围\r\n16.7以下(包含16.7)\t低体重\t\r\n16.7以上23.7以下(包含23.7)\t正常\r\n23.7以上26.5以下(包含26.5)\t超重\r\n26.5 以上\t肥胖\r\n【操作要求】\r\n(1)补充完善程序中画线处的内容，使程序语句完整。\r\n(2)找出程序中的两处错误并改正。\r\n(3)运行程序，输入身高和体重，测试输出结果是否正确。\r\n### 代码调试区 提示\r\n4.请注意，代码中需添了缺失的变量w，它代表用户的体重。此外，要修正if语句中的条件，以确保所有可能的BMI值都被正确分类。\r\n\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\nh = float(input("请输入你的身高(单位:m)"))   # 请求用户输入身高（单位：米），并将输入值转换为浮点数\r\n__ = float(input("请输入你的体重(单位:kg)"))   # 请求用户输入体重（单位：千克），并将输入值转换为浮点数\r\nBMI = w/h**2  # 计算相应的BMI值   # 使用体重除以身高的平方来计算BMI值\r\nBMI = round(BMI, 1)  # 对变量BMI的值进行四舍五入后，保留1位小数   # 将BMI值四舍五入到小数点后一位\r\nprint("你的身体质量指数是：", BMI)   # 输出用户的BMI值\r\nif BMI___16.7:   # 根据BMI值判断用户的体重等级，如果BMI小于16.7\r\n    print("BMI等级:低体重")  # 输出低体重等级\r\nelif BMI < 23.7:   # 如果BMI在16.7到23.7之间（不包含23.7）\r\n    print("BMI等级:正常")  # 输出正常等级\r\nelif BMI > 26.5:   # 如果BMI大于26.5\r\n    print("BMI 等级:超重")  # 输出超重等级\r\nelse:  # 否则\r\n    print("BMI等级:肥胖")   # 如果BMI在23.7到26.5之间，输出肥胖等级\r\n```\r\n### 答案\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\nh = float(input("请输入你的身高(单位:m)"))   # 请求用户输入身高（单位：米），并将输入值转换为浮点数\r\nw = float(input("请输入你的体重(单位:kg)"))   # 请求用户输入体重（单位：千克），并将输入值转换为浮点数          #填空1\r\nBMI = w/h**2  # 计算相应的BMI值   # 使用体重除以身高的平方来计算BMI值\r\nBMI = round(BMI, 1)  # 对变量BMI的值进行四舍五入后，保留1位小数   # 将BMI值四舍五入到小数点后一位\r\nprint("你的身体质量指数是：", BMI)   # 输出用户的BMI值\r\nif BMI <= 16.7:   # 根据BMI值判断用户的体重等级，如果BMI小于等于16.7                                         #填空2\r\n    print("BMI等级:低体重")  # 输出低体重等级\r\nelif BMI <= 23.7:   # 如果BMI在16.7到23.7之间（包含23.7）                                                  #错误1\r\n    print("BMI等级:正常")  # 如果BMI在23.7到26.5之间，输出肥胖等级\r\n```\r\n## 题目5\r\n### 4) 表示循环将执行从1到3的数字，总共3次。if 语句中使用 and 来确保用户名和密码都必须正确才能登录成功。如果计数器 count 达到或超过3，表示用户已经连续三次输入错误，程序将输出相应的提示信息。\r\n\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\nuname = "张萌"  # 该变量预先保存用户名   # 预设的用户名\r\nupass = "123456"   # 预设的密码\r\ncount = 0  # 计数器，记录用户输入错误的次数   # 初始化计数器，用于记录用户输入错误的次数\r\nfor i in range(1, ___):   # 构造一个最多执行3次的循环\r\n    sname = input("请输人用户名:")    # 请求用户输入用户名\r\n    spass = input("请输人密码:")   # 如果不匹配，打印错误信息\r\n    count += 1  # 增加计数器\r\nif count > 3:      # 如果用户输入错误达到3次，打印不允许登录的信息\r\n    print("输入错误达3次,不允许登录!")  # 出错3次，退出程序\r\n```\r\n### 答案\r\n``` py linenums="1"\r\nuname = "张萌"  # 该变量预先保存用户名   # 预设的用户名\r\nupass = "123456"   # 预设的密码\r\ncount = 0  # 计数器，记录用户输入错误的次数   # 初始化计数器，用于记录用户输入错误的次数\r\nfor i in range(1, 4):   # 构造一个最多执行3次的循环 print("登录成功!")   # 如果匹配打印登录成功\r\n        break  # 退出循环\r\n    else:  # 否则                                                                                   #错误1\r\n        print("用户名或密码错误!")   # dir="auto">编程实现人机互动猜1到100之间整数游戏。<br>\n计算机随机生成一个数，游戏参与人有5次猜数机会，每次在程序界面输入所猜的数，游戏程序会给予相应提示“猜大了！”或“猜小了！”<br>\n猜对游戏直接结束，如5次尝试都不对游戏结束。请你阅读下列程序段，根据题意，实现游戏功能。<br>\n【操作要求】</p>\n<ol dir="auto">\n<li>补充完善下划线处的内容使程序语句完整。(下划线须删去)</li>\n<li>找出程序中的两处错误并改正。</li>\n<li>运行程序，输入你猜的数字，验证结果是否正确。</li>\n</ol>\n<h3 dir="auto">代码调试区 <a 每人三次机会，<br>\n需有两次连续垫球次数满35次(包含35)方能通过考核。请你完善程序，输入学生每次的垫球次数，并判断是否通过考核。<br>\n【操作要示】<br>\n(1)补充完善下划线处的内容使程序语句完整<br>\n(2)找出程序中的两处错误并改正<br>\n(3)运行程序，当依次输入每次的垫球次数为36、32、38时，检查输出结果是否为"恭喜你，已通过考核!”。</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">身体质量指数(BMI)是衡量人体肥胖程度的重要标准，其计算方法为身体质量指数等于体重(单位:kg)除以身高(单位:m)的平方。<br>\n下表是高二男生BMI数值对应的等级表。请你根据提供的程序段输入身高和体重，计算BMI的值并判断BMI的等级。<br>\nBMI数值范围\tBMI数值范围<br>\n16.7以下(包含16.7)\t低体重<br>\n16.7以上23.7以下(包含23.7)\t正常<br>\n23.7以上26.5以下(包含26.5)\t超重<br>\n26.5 以上\t肥胖<br>\n【操作要求】<br>\n(1)补充完善程序中画线处的内容，使程序语句完整。<br>\n(2)找出程序中的两处错误并改正。<br>\n(3)运行程序，输入身高和体重，测试输出结果是否正确。</p>\n<h3 dir="auto">代码调试区 <a href="https://pythontutor.com/visualize.html#code=h%3Dfloat%28input%28%22%E8%AF%B7%E8%BE%93%E5%85%A5%E4%BD%A0%E7%9A%84%E8%BA%AB%E9%AB%98%28%E5%8D%95%E4%BD%8D%3Am%29%22%29%29%0A__%3Dfloat%28input%28%22%E8%AF%B7%E8%BE%93%E5%85%A5%E4%BD%A0%E7%9A%84%E4%BD%93%E9%87%8D%28%E5%8D%95%E4%BD%8D%3Akg%29%22%29%29%0ABMI%3Dw/h**2%20%20%23%20%E8%AE%A1%E7%AE%97%E7%9B%B8%E5%BA%94%E7%9A%84BMI%E5%80%BC%0ABMI%3Dround%28BMI,%201%29%20%20%23%20%E5%AF%B9%E5%8F%98%E9%87%8FBMI%E7%9A%84%E5%80%BC%E8%BF%9B%E8%A1%8C%E5%9B%9B%E8%88%8D%E4%BA%94%E5%85%A5%E5%90%8E%EF%BC%8C%E4%BF%9D%E7%95%991%E4%BD%8D%E5%B0%8F%E6%95%B0%0Aprint%28%22%E4%BD%A0%E7%9A%84%E8%BA%AB%E4%BD%93%E8%B4%A8%E9%87%8F%E6%8C%87%E6%95%B0%E6%98%AF%EF%BC%9A%22,BMI%29%0Aif%20BMI___16.7%3A%0A%20%20%20%20print%28%22BMI%E7%AD%89%E7%BA%A7%3A%E4%BD%8E%E4%BD%93%E9%87%8D%22%29%0Aelif%20%20BMI%3C23.7%3A%0A%20%20%20%20print%28%22BMI%E7%AD%89%E7%BA%A7%3A%E6%AD%A3%E5%B8%B8%22%29%0Aelif%20BMI%3E26.5%3A%0A%20%20%20%20print%28%22BMI%20%E7%AD%89%E7%BA%A7%3A%E8%B6%85%E9%87%8D%22%29%0Aelse%3A%0A%20%20%20%20print%28%22BMI%E7%AD%89%E7%BA%A7%3A%E8%82%A5%E8%83%96%22%29&amp;cumulative=false&amp;heapPrimitives=nevernest&amp;mode=edit&amp;origin=opt-frontend.js&amp;py=3&amp;rawInputLstJSON=%5B%5D&amp;textReferences=false" dir="auto">提示</h3>\n<p dir="auto">4.请注意，代码中需添了缺失的变量w，它代表用户的体重。此外，要修正if语句中的条件，以确保所有可能的BMI值都被正确分类。</p>\n<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto">用户身份认证是各类信息系统的一个重要功能模块，<br>\n现运用Python程序模拟用户登录，<br>\n通过输入用户名和密码，实现用户身份验证。<br>\n即输入用户名和密码，如果正确，显示欢迎信息，否则输出错误信息。<br>\n若连续输入达3次错误，则不能继续登录。程序代码如下:<br>\n【操作要求】<br>\n(1)补充完善程序中画线处的内容，使程序语句完整。(2)找出程序中的两处错误并改正。<br>\n(3)运行程序，输入一组数据，测试输出结果是否正确。<br>\n用户名:张萌，密码:123456，输出:登录成功!<br>\n输人错误信息，<br>\n输出:用户名或密码错误!输入错误信息三次，<br>\n输出:输入错误达3次，不允许登录!</p>\n<h3 来确保用户名和密码都必须正确才能登录成功。如果计数器 count 达到或超过3，表示用户已经连续三次输入错误，程序将输出相应的提示信息。</li>\n</ol>\n<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="uname = &quot;张萌&quot;  # 该变量预先保存用户名   # 预设的用户名\nupass = &quot;123456&quot;   # 预设的密码\ncount = 0  # 计数器，记录用户输入错误的次数   # 初始化计数器，用于记录用户输入错误的次数\nfor i in range(1, count &gt; 3:      # 如果用户输入错误达到3次，打印不允许登录的信息\n    print(&quot;输入错误达3次,不允许登录!&quot;)  # 出错3次，退出程序"><pre class="notranslate"><span class="pl-s1">uname</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span 预设的密码</span>\n<span class="pl-s1">count</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">0</span>  <span class="pl-c"># 计数器，记录用户输入错误的次数   # 初始化计数器，用于记录用户输入错误的次数</span>\n<span #填空2</span>\n        <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s">"登录成功!"</span>)   <span class="pl-c"># 如果匹配打印登录成功</span>\n        <span class="pl-k">break</span>  <span class="pl-c">#
